        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974"><meta property="og:title" content="Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced d-p Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.3c02974.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion battery (SIB) is a reasonable alternative to lithium-ion battery (LIB) in the field of grid-scale energy storage systems. Unfortunately, the development of appropriate cathode material is a bottleneck in the field of SIB. In the present work, (p-TQ)-VO, formulated as (p-TQ)0.2V2O5·0.38H2O, was synthesized based on a facile hydrothermal reaction of V2O5 and methylhydroquinone (p-HTQ). And when V2O5 was replaced by VN, (p-TQ)-VN, formulated as (p-TQ)0.22V2(O/N)5, was prepared instead. The (p-TQ)-VO sample displays good electrochemical performance as the SIB cathode. And (p-TQ)-VN shows a much higher capacity at a small current density, and it can maintain structural integrity with partial topotactic phase transformation into NaxV2O5 during the discharge/charge process. A series of characterizations of (p-TQ)-VO and (p-TQ)-VN reveals the successful intercalation of p-TQ into the layered V2O5 with a (001) lattice spacing of 13.7 and 10.7 Å, respectively. In (p-TQ)-VN, partial terminal oxygen (Ot) atoms from the V–O–V layer have been substituted by N atoms, which can boost the orbital hybridization of V 3d and Ot 2p, shorten the V–Ot bonds in the c-axial direction, and elongate the V–O bonds in the ab plane with compressed {VO4N2} octahedra, giving rise to mixed-anion coordination effect. As a result, the enhanced electron densities around the Ot atoms of the V–O–V layer can facilitate the affinity toward the inserted Na+ ions, leading to partial phase conversion into NaNO2/NaNO3. Moreover, density functional density (DFT) calculations reveal that the N-incorporation can improve electron conductivity with richer molecular orbital energy levels, resulting in multistep redox reactions and enhanced capacity."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/acs.inorgchem.3c02974.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187d735b8cd404-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187d735b8cd404-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '44ae9870-bfd2-493a-8c94-4549de8f5d79'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187d735b8cd404-MAN">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'inorganic_chemistry';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/inorganic_chemistry/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="93187d735b8cd404-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "inocaj";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });

                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced &lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;d&lt;/italic&gt;-&lt;italic toggle&#x3D;&quot;yes&quot;&gt;p&lt;/italic&gt; Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});

        </div></div><div class="article__pubdate"><span>Article</span><time datetime="2023-11-21">November 21, 2023</time></div><h1 class="article_header-title" tabindex="0"><span class="hlFld-Title" property="headline">Organic Species-Intercalated Vanadium Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery: Mixed-Anion Coordination Effect, Enhanced <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> Orbital Hybridization, and Topotactic Phase Conversion Induced by N-Substitution</span><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy article link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Article link copied!</span></div></h1><ul class="loa"><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Kai Li</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Kai Li</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Department of Applied Chemistry, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Chongqing University, Chongqing 401331, P. class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">3</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion battery (SIB) is a reasonable alternative to lithium-ion battery (LIB) in the field of grid-scale energy storage systems. Unfortunately, the development of appropriate cathode material is a bottleneck in the field of SIB. In the present work, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, formulated as (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·0.38H<sub>2</sub>O, was synthesized based on a facile hydrothermal reaction of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and methylhydroquinone (<i>p</i>-HTQ). And when V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was replaced by VN, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, formulated as (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub>, was prepared instead. The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO sample displays good electrochemical performance as the SIB cathode. And (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows a much higher capacity at a small current density, and it can maintain structural integrity with partial topotactic phase transformation into Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the discharge/charge process. A series of characterizations of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN reveals the successful intercalation of <i>p</i>-TQ into the layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with a (001) lattice spacing of 13.7 and 10.7 Å, respectively. In (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, partial terminal oxygen (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms from the V–O–V layer have been substituted by N atoms, which can boost the orbital hybridization of V 3<i>d</i> and O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i>, shorten the V–O<sub>t</sub> bonds in the <i>c</i>-axial direction, and elongate the V–O bonds in the <i>ab</i> plane with compressed {VO<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>} octahedra, giving rise to mixed-anion coordination effect. As a result, the enhanced electron densities around the O<sub>t</sub> atoms of the V–O–V layer can facilitate the affinity toward the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> ions, leading to partial phase conversion into NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>. Moreover, density functional density (DFT) calculations reveal that the N-incorporation can improve electron conductivity with richer molecular orbital energy levels, resulting in multistep redox reactions and enhanced capacity.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D20105%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DKai%2BLi%252C%2BChanglin%2BLiu%252C%2BJia%2BLv%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D62%26issueNum%3D49%26contentID%3Dacs.inorgchem.3c02974%26title%3DOrganic%2BSpecies-Intercalated%2BVanadium%2BOxide%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%253A%2BMixed-Anion%2BCoordination%2BEffect%252C%2BEnhanced%2Bd-p%2BOrbital%2BHybridization%252C%2Band%2BTopotactic%2BPhase%2BConversion%2BInduced%2Bby%2BN-Substitution%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0020-1669%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dinocaj%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D20119%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291484&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffraction"> Diffraction </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291146&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Lattices"> Lattices </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291719&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Redox reactions"> Redox reactions </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="hlFld-Abstract synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><span><p class="articleBody_abstractText">An N-substituted methylquinone-intercalated vanadium oxide displays a much improved capacity at a smaller current density than its free-N counterpart due to the enhanced <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization and mixed-anion coordination effect.</p></span></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In recent years, due to the intrinsic advantages of abundant Na resource, low cost, and similar physical and chemical properties as lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have captured widespread attention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, due to the larger radius and heavier molar mass of Na<sup>+</sup> than Li<sup>+</sup>, the practical performances of SIBs are limited by suitable electrode materials, especially for cathode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> Among the promising cathode materials, layered vanadium-based materials such as V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (high theoretical capacity: 589 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) have attracted more and more interest due to the advantages of abundant sources and low cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6−9)</a> More importantly, the interlayer spacing of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> can be enlarged by preinserting metal ions, which can not only expose more interstitial sites for the (de)intercalation of lithium or sodium ions but also accelerate the reaction kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> A series of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (M = Li, Na, K, Mg, Ca) with expanded interlayer spacings have been reported, which can achieve high initial capacity and superior capacity retention in LIBs or SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 12 to 15">(12−15)</a> Organic compounds can also be employed as interlayer pillars. For example, the polyaniline (PANI)-intercalation strategy was reported to relieve the electrostatic interactions between Zn<sup>2+</sup> and lattice oxygen of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, thus accelerating the kinetics of Zn<sup>2+</sup> transportation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Moreover, preinserting redox-active organic quinone-type molecules into layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> can also improve the capacities due to the keto-phenol conversion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Although encouraging achievements have been made, there is still much room to improve the electrochemical performances of vanadium-based cathode materials in SIBs. Though the introduction of disorder/defect can improve electron conductivity and promote the storage capability, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> it would decrease the crystallinity of the material. What is worse, during the discharge/charge process, with the (de)intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, the cathode materials usually become more and more amorphous. Therefore, no diffraction peaks can be observed in the <i>in situ</i> or <i>ex situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, which is not beneficial for the exact identification of the discharge/charge mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> And generally, it is contradictory to achieve high crystallinity and good storage capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to solve the above issues, in the present work, first, we synthesized an oxygen-deficient (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·0.38H<sub>2</sub>O, denoted as (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, through a facile hydrothermal reaction of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and methylhydroquinone (<i>p</i>-HTQ), in which <i>p</i>-HTQ was oxidized by V<sub>2</sub><sup>V</sup>O<sub>5</sub> into methylquinone (<i>p</i>-TQ) (R-C–OH + V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> → R-C═O + V<sub><i>x</i></sub><sup>IV</sup>V<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub><sup>V</sup>O<sub>5</sub>), and <i>p</i>-TQ was simultaneously inserted into the layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with an enlarged interlayer spacing of ∼13.7 Å. The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO sample exhibits a good electrochemical behavior in SIB. Second, we obtained a N-incorporated counterpart, (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub>, denoted as (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, by utilizing VN as the starting material instead of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> under hydrothermal conditions. Herein, VN was chosen due to the good electron conductivity of the metal nitride. On the other hand, it is expected that the incorporation of an alien element (N) into a vanadium compound can produce disorders and defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> The resultant N-substituted product, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, shows a remarkably enhanced initial capacity. It should be mentioned that in order to obtain high crystalline VN, in the present work, VN was synthesized by sintering and nitriding V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> by melamine at a high temperature of 800 °C in an Ar atmosphere. Please see section S1 and Figure S1 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> (SI) for details.</div><div class="NLM_p last">During the subsequent hydrothermal reaction of VN and <i>p</i>-HTQ, with the attack of oxidants such as H<sub>2</sub>O, VN was transformed into N-substituted V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> accompanied by the insertion of the organic species, as evidenced by three-dimensional (3D) continuous rotation electron diffraction (cRED), Rietveld refinement, and high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF)–scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM). And the high crystallinity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN makes it possible to reveal the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism by common <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns. It is found that (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN can maintain structural integrity with a partial topotactic transformation into Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the discharge/charge process. And (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows much higher capacity than (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO at a small current density, which is due to partial terminal oxygen (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms of the V–O–V layer substituted by N atoms. The N-substitution can boost the orbital hybridization of V 3<i>d</i> and O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i>, shorten the V–O<sub>t</sub> bonds in the <i>c</i>-axial direction, and elongate the V–O bonds in the <i>ab</i> plane with compressed {VO<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>} octahedron, giving rise to mixed-anion coordination effect and a shrinkage of interlayer spacing in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (10.7 Å). As a result, the enhanced electron densities around the O<sub>t</sub> atoms of the V–O–V layer can facilitate the affinity toward the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> ions with more negative binding energy, leading to partial phase conversion into NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>. In addition, density functional density (DFT) calculations unveil that the N-substitution can improve electron conductivity with richer molecular orbital energy levels, thus resulting in multistep redox reactions and enhanced capacity.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Compositions and Structures of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN</h3><div class="NLM_p">As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN exhibit the strongest diffraction peaks at 6.6 and 8.3°, respectively, corresponding to their (001) crystalline plane with lattice spacings of 13.7 and 10.7 Å, respectively, which are larger than those of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (4.4 Å, PDF#72-0433) and VN (2.4 Å, PDF#78-1315), which can be attributed to the incorporation of the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species into the layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. First, the symmetry and unit cell of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN were investigated by the 3D cRED technique. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c, according to the 3D reciprocal lattice reconstructed from the cRED result, a triclinic unit cell can be identified (<i>a</i> = 12.2652 Å, <i>b</i> = 3.7054 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.5408 Å, α = 90°, β = 101.171°, γ = 90°). Based on the preliminary cell parameters, Rietveld refinements of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·0.38H<sub>2</sub>O and (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> were performed. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the Rietveld refined XRD patterns of the two samples all disclose that the calculated diffraction profiles fit well with the corresponding experimental results. The reliability factors for (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO are <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 7.01% and <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 7.37% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a), and as for (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 5.35% and <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 5.98% are obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN samples both crystallize in <i>P</i><sub>1̅</sub> space group, their unit cell parameters are <i>a</i> = 11.946/12.325 Å, <i>b</i> = 3.706/3.736 Å, <i>c</i> = 13.710/10.693 Å, α = 90.485/89.918°, β = 99.129/101.590°, γ = 90.333/90.410°, and V = 599.24/482.32 Å<sup>3</sup>. The low <i>P</i><sub>1̅</sub> symmetry of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (CCDC <a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structure_request?pid=ccdc:2277626&amp;issn=0020-1669&amp;id=doi:10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;sid=ACS">2277626</a>) and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (CCDC <a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structure_request?pid=ccdc:2277625&amp;issn=0020-1669&amp;id=doi:10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;sid=ACS">2277625</a>) is ascribed to the insertion of organic species. The large size and steric hindrance of <i>p</i>-TQ make it impossible to occupy all of the symmetrical sites in the interlayer space of vanadium oxide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> And most of the crystallographically independent atoms are disordered over two positions.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Rietveld refinements of (a) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (b) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN; (c) cRED data of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN: (left) the overall 3D reciprocal lattice and the reciprocal lattice patterns viewed along the <i>a*-</i>, <i>b*-</i>, and <i>c*</i>-axis, respectively; (d) structural comparison of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, the apical V–O distances in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (2.718–2.767 Å) are longer than those in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (2.104–2.197 Å) (highlighted in red circles), which arises from the larger interlayer spacing of the (001) facet in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (13.7 Å) than that in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (10.7 Å). As a result, the V–O–V bilayer in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is split into two separate V–O–V monolayers with the (001) facet as the division plane in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO. Therefore, the V centers in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO are four- or five-coordinated by oxygen atoms to furnish tetrahedral (<i>T</i><sub>d</sub>) or square pyramidal (P<sub><i>y</i></sub>) geometries (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, left), whereas in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, they are five- or six-coordinated by O atoms in distorted P<sub><i>y</i></sub> and octahedral (O<sub>h</sub>) geometries (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, right).</div><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, it is worth noting that the terminal oxygen (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms (highlighted in sapphire) of the V–O–V layer are partially substituted by N atoms in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), leading to the shrinkage of the V–O<sub>t</sub> bond lengths (average value: 1.537 Å) in the c-axial direction compared with those in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (average value: 1.867 Å). On the contrary, the N-substitution gives rise to the slight elongation of the V–O<sub>b</sub> bonds (average values: 2.086 Å in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN; 1.888 Å in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO) in the <i>ab</i> plane (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, O<sub>b</sub> represents bridging oxygen, highlighted in light orange) due to the expansion of <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> lattice parameters in the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN cell (<i>a</i> = 12.325 Å, <i>b</i> = 3.736 Å) compared with those in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (<i>a</i> = 11.946 Å, <i>b</i> = 3.706 Å).</div><div class="NLM_p">Therefore, N-incorporation and the resultant shrinkage of interlayer spacing (10.7 Å) lead to compressed {V(O/N)<sub>6</sub>} octahedra in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, which include two shorter bonds in the <i>c</i>-axis direction and four longer bonds in the ab plane in comparison with those in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO. The mixed-anion coordination effect is also observed in previous works. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 24 to 27">(24−27)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN samples were further characterized by an infrared spectrum (IR). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the IR spectra of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN all display the vibration peaks at ∼550, 765, and 1010 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are similar to those of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> indicating the successful transformation of VN into V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the hydrothermal synthesis of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. In addition, the two samples show the vibrations of the benzene ring (C═C/C–C) in the range of 1200–1800 cm<sup>–1</sup>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> which stem from the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species. And the peaks at ∼1635 and 1400 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be attributed to the antisymmetric (ν<sub>as</sub>) and symmetric (ν<sub>s</sub>) vibrations of C═O functional group in <i>p</i>-TQ, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> The results prove that <i>p</i>-TQ is successfully preinserted into the layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The contents of N (obsd: 4.9 wt %, calc: 4.7 wt %) and O in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (obsd: 36.2 wt %, calc: 36.5 wt %) were determined by a combustion analyzer (EMGA-830, Horiba), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> suggesting the molar ratio of O/N is ∼6.1.</div><div class="NLM_p">CH elemental analyses were also conducted to further determine the compositions of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. The C contents in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (obsd: 8.4 wt %, calc: 8.9 wt %) and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (obsd: 8.8%, calc: 8.9%) match with the stoichiometric formulas of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·0.38H<sub>2</sub>O and (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4.3</sub>N<sub>0.7</sub>, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, thermogravimetric (TG) measurement was performed to reveal the content of each component in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Please see section S2 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> for details.</div><div class="NLM_p">As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4a</a>, the as-synthesized (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO displays an accordion-like morphology, which is constructed by orderly stacked nanosheets, whereas the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN sample presents a flower-like morphology in which the nanobelts are arranged in a divergent mode due to the inclusion of N element (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b</a>). In conclusion, compared with the irregular rock-like morphology of the commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN both exhibit fluffy morphologies, which is beneficial for the penetration of the electrolyte into the active sites and shorten the diffusion pathway of Na<sup>+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN and then giving rise to an enhanced electrochemical performance.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (b) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN; (c–h) XPS spectra comparison of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN: (c) survey; (d) V 2p; (e) O 1s; (f) C 1s and (g) N 1s; (h) EPR spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The corresponding EDS and elemental mappings of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c,d</a>, respectively. It can be seen that the elements are evenly distributed in the samples. And the large amount of N content (18.81 at %) in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN further proves the inclusion of N into the organic species-inserted vanadium oxide.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to gain insight into the chemical states of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. The XPS full spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) of the two samples reveal the presence of V, C, and O signals in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and the presence of V, C, O, and N signals in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, which agree well with the above EDS results.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>(d–g) further shows the high-resolution XPS spectra of V 2p, O 1s, C 1s, and N 1s. As exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, the V 2p fine spectra of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN both display two pairs of characteristic peaks of V<sup>4+</sup> (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>: 516.1 eV; V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>: 523.1 eV) and V<sup>5+</sup> (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>: 517.4 eV; V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>: 524.7 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> It is worth noting that the molar ratios of V<sup>5+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN are ca. 4.45 and 2.30, respectively, indicating more amount of V<sup>4+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN due to the inclusion of N.</div><div class="NLM_p">The O 1s spectrum of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e) can be divided into four obvious peaks located at ∼530.2 eV (V–O), ∼531.4 eV (oxygen defect), ∼532.0 eV (C═O), and ∼533.1 eV (C–O–H). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> In (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, there are also four similar peaks. It is worth mentioning that the characteristic peak of ∼530.0 eV (V–O) in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is a bit negatively shifted in comparison with that in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, which can be attributed to the higher ratio of V<sup>4+</sup> and the increased electron density around the V centers in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. Moreover, the amount of oxygen defect (O<sub>d</sub>) in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (7.4%) is more than that (2.7 at %) in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, which may offer higher specific capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f, the C 1s spectra of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN can both be separated into three peaks at ∼284.8, ∼ 286.4, and ∼288.2 eV, corresponding to the C–C/C═C, C–O, and C═O bonds of <i>p</i>-TQ, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 and 39">(37,39)</a> The presence of N in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is also verified by the N 1s spectrum. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g, no N signal is detected in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, whereas two N signals can be observed in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, which are assigned to V–N–V (400.3 eV) and V = N (401.4 eV), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> suggesting that the O atoms of the V–O–V layer in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO are partially replaced by N atoms in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN.</div><div class="NLM_p">Since the unpaired electrons at the defective sites can generate a corresponding signal, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> the presence of oxygen vacancies in the two samples can be further demonstrated by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectroscopy. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN both exhibit a sharp symmetric peak at <i>g</i> = 1.97, and the EPR signal in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is stronger than that in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, illustrating more oxygen vacancies in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, which is consistent with their O 1s spectra.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to gain further insight into the microstructure of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO were performed. It is found that (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO also displays a morphology of nanosheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a), which is in agreement with the SEM images. The periphery of the nanosheet (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, the cyan square in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b) exhibits lattice fringes with <i>d</i> spacings of 1.03 and 0.35 nm as well as a dihedral angle of ca. 76°, which correspond to the (0 0 3/4) and (110) planes of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, respectively. Similarly, the central region of the nanosheet (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d, the red square in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b) shows similar lattice fringes of the (00<i>l</i>) and (110) facets. However, it can be observed that the (002) (<i>d</i> = 0.69 nm) lattice fringe is divided into smaller lattice fringes of (003) (<i>d =</i> 0.46 nm) and (006) (<i>d</i> = 0.23 nm) facets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Furthermore, large amounts of crystalline defects are observed in the nanosheet (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), which probably can act as active sites for the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> to improve the capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> The prominent spots in the SAED pattern can be well indexed to (002), (200), (110), (005), (008), (009), and (0 0 12) planes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), consistent with the XRD pattern of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b, e) TEM, (c, d, f) HRTEM images of (a, b) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (e, f) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN; (g) HAADF-STEM image of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (inset: FFT); (h) Magnified image from the pink square in panel (g), highlighting the projection spacings of V···V; (i) structural diagram of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN with the V···V projection distances marked based on the HAADF-STEM image.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The microstructure of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN was also characterized by TEM and HRTEM. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows a nanobelt-like morphology, which is consistent with the SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b</a>). However, the nanobelts are built from interconnected nanorods (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). Distinguished lattice fringes with <i>d</i> spacings of ∼1.07 nm are observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f), corresponding to the (001) facet. It can be seen that such fringes are not well arranged with dislocation and disorder, which is due to the intercalation of organic species and the doping of the N element. HAADF-STEM was further performed viewed down [001] direction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g, inset). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g, an ordered atomic array is observed in the HAADF-STEM image of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. And it should be mentioned that vacancies and disorders are also observed in the HAADF-STEM image, which is ascribed to the incorporation of organic species and N element. The distances between two neighboring V centers are ∼2.0, 2.0, and 2.8 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h), which matches well with the V···V projection distances in the 2D V–O–V layer of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i). The result is similar to that in the free-N counterpart reported by our previous work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Performances of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the electrochemical performances of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN as the cathode materials in SIBs, first, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN were dehydrated under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at 200 °C for 1 h (for details, see section S3 and Figure S7 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance reveals that the dehydrated (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO possesses superior rate capacities of 247, 209, 185, 163, 144, 126, and 105 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and after switching the current density back to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a high and reversible capacity of 245 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be restored, which is much higher than that of the commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> counterpart (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), suggesting the much better Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO. Impressively, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows a higher average specific capacity than (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO at the same current density, which is 332, 259, 232, 213, and 181 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. And when the current density returns to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity can reach 272 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). Unfortunately, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is very prone to short circuits when the current density exceeds 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>; thus, it is unable to further investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism at higher current densities.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Rate capabilities and (b, c) cycling performances of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, and the commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> at (b) 0.1 and (c) 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>; the first three CV curves of (d) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (e) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the cycling performances of the two dehydrated samples were investigated at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode can deliver a satisfactory specific capacity of ∼200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) cycles, and the capacity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO is higher than that of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the whole GCD process. It is due to the expansion of interlayer spacing caused by the preinsertion of the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species, which is beneficial for the (de)intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. However, the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode exhibits a different cycling behavior; its specific capacity gradually decays from an initial value of 243 to 143 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles with a capacity retention of only 58.8%. Though the initial capacity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is much higher than those of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, the fast capacity fading indicates that the N-inclusion can lead to worse cycling performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Significantly, at a higher current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO still exhibits good long-term durability. The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO sample can deliver a capacity of 101 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a retention of 73.7% over 1400 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), which is also much higher than that of the commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the whole cycling test at the higher current density, suggesting that the intercalation of <i>p</i>-TQ can accelerate the reaction kinetics. Because the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode is very prone to short circuits at larger current density (&gt;1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>), its cycling performance at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> is not measured. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO is comparable to and even surpasses those of the previously reported vanadium-based cathode materials in SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to explain the reason for the different Na<sup>+</sup> storage behaviors, the first three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> have been measured. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>(d,e), regardless of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, the initial CV curve is different from those of the subsequent two profiles. And their respective second and third CV curves are almost overlapped, indicating that the two samples become stable after the first CV process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO exhibits discernible two redox couples at ∼1.6/2.0 and 2.5/3.0 vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The peaks at ∼1.6/2.0 V correspond to the V<sup>3+</sup> ↔ V<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> And another pair of peaks might be assigned to V<sup>4+</sup> ↔ V<sup>5+</sup> and/or the conversion of C═O ↔ C–O in <i>p</i>-TQ. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref46" aria-label="reference 10 and 46">(10,46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows more obvious redox responses than (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO. In the first cycle, it can be seen that the pair of redox couple in the potential range of 1.4–2.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (V<sup>3+</sup>↔ V<sup>4+</sup> conversion) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> is split into two pairs of peaks, which is due to the richer orbital energy levels in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN caused by N-substitution; the details will be discussed in the section of DFT simulation. Additionally, the extra oxidization peaks in the high potential of 3.6–3.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na are associated with the transformation of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN → NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>; the details will be discussed in the section of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">ex situ XRD</a>. Since partial terminal oxygen (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms are substituted by N atoms in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, the formation of NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub> means the loss of lattice N atoms, leading to the creation of O/N vacancies in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. The loss of lattice O generally occurs in the high voltage, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> indicating the simultaneous generation of NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub> and oxygen/nitrogen defects in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a consequence, the CV shapes in the second and third cycles in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN are discernibly different from that in the first cycle. The redox peaks of V<sup>3+</sup>↔ V<sup>4+</sup> become sharper, and three pairs of redox couples with major peaks are observed at ∼2.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e), indicating a multistep and complex Na<sup>+</sup> storage process (including V<sup>4+</sup> ↔ V<sup>5+</sup> transformation), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref45" aria-label="reference 10 and 45">(10,45)</a> which are related with the oxygen vacancies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 47 to 49">(47−49)</a> Moreover, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN shows a pair of extra redox peaks at 3.56/3.61 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, which corresponds to the redox reaction of oxygen vacancies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> The more multistep redox mechanism in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN can account for the higher capacity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN than (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO at a small current density.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The GCD curves of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN were also carried out (for details, please see section S4 and Figure S8 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Kinetics of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN</h3><div class="NLM_p">The multiscanned CVs of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN at different scan rates were carried out to investigate their electrochemical kinetics in SIBs, which are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>(a–d) and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9</a>, respectively. The relationship between peak current (<i>i</i>) and sweep rate (ν), <i>i = av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, can be used to estimate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> in the cathode materials. As is well known, the value of <i>b</i> is 0.5, indicating that the capacity is controlled completely by the diffusion-controlled process, while a value of 1.0 for <i>b</i> indicates a fully capacitive process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b, the <i>b</i>-value of the four redox peaks in the CVs of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO is 0.86–0.89, indicating that the electrochemical reaction is mainly a capacitive process. In addition, the capacity can be split into capacitive (<i>i</i><sub>cap</sub>, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled (<i>i</i><sub>diff</sub>, <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) processes at each scan rate based on the equation: <i>i</i> = <i>i</i><sub>cap</sub> + <i>i</i><sub>diff</sub> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v + k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, at a sweep rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the shaded area represents the capacitive contribution to the total capacity, which contributes 78.1% of the total capacity, resulting in the superior rate capability of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO. And the capacitive contribution increases from 54.8 to 81.3% as the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 1.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical kinetics analysis of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO: (a) CV curves at different scan rates; (b) log (<i>i</i>) vs log (<i>v</i>) plots obtained from CVs; (c) capacitive contribution at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (d) contribution ratios of the pseudocapacities at different scan rates; (e) GITT profiles and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (f) EIS spectra for (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical kinetics of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN were also investigated. As aforementioned, the CV shape of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is much different from that of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, and more redox peaks are observed in the CVs of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9a</a>). The <i>b</i> values of the redox peaks in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN are in the range of 0.75–0.98 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>), indicating it is mainly a capacitive process. And the capacitive contributions increase from 80.9 to 93.6% when the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 1.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c,d</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was utilized to monitor the dynamic behavior of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, including a galvanostatic period and a relaxation session of 15 and 30 min at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.
