As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e, the diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO are in the range of 10<sup>–8</sup>–10<sup>–7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which are comparable and even superior to those in the previously reported cathode materials in SIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref51" aria-label="reference 12 and 51">(12,51)</a> indicating the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Furthermore, the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, and commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> were measured comparatively. Their Nyquist plots are shown <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f; the charge-transfer impedances (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) follow the sequence as follows: (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (8.4 Ω cm<sup>–2</sup>) &lt; (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (14.8 Ω cm<sup>–2</sup>) &lt; V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (19.2 Ω cm<sup>–2</sup>) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), indicating that (<i>p</i>-BQ)-VN has the fastest charge-transfer kinetics.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> <i>Ex Situ</i> Characterizations of (<i>p</i>-BQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-BQ)-VN during the First Discharge and Charge Process</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to explore the origin of the excellent performance of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode in SIB, <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns at different voltage states during the first discharge and discharge process were recorded. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a, (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO exhibits its strongest (001) diffraction crystalline plane only in the presence of the carbon additives and the current collector of aluminum foil. And during the first discharge and charge process, the (001) diffraction peak almost disappears, which can be ascribed to the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, resulting in the disordered (001) facet. Only upon the first charge to 3.8 V, this reflection peak of the (001) plane reappears again but with much weakened intensity, suggesting the partial recovery of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode at the first fully charged state. No new phase was detected during the discharge/charge process, which is due to the decreased crystallinity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO caused by the continuous insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and the resultant disorders/defects in the sample.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. <i>Ex situ</i> XRD patterns of (a) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (d, e) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrodes. (b) V K-edge XANES of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, V foil, and the 1st fully discharged and charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrodes; (c) Fourier transforms of the V K-edge EXAFS in <i>R</i> space.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to gain more information on the amorphous (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO during the first discharge and charge process, we measured the V K-edge X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption spectroscopy (EXAFS) of the first fully discharged and charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrodes. For comparison, the XANES of the pristine (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO powder, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and V foil were also measured.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b, the main edge feature of the pristine (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO powder is similar to that of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which is completely different from that of the V foil. That is because the intercalation of the <i>p</i>-TQ species into V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> does not change the fundamental host framework of the parental V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> phase. The main edge of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO shows a slightly negative shift in comparison with that of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, suggesting the presence of dominant V<sup>5+</sup> and less amount of V<sup>4+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, which is in accordance with the V 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). The pre-edge intensity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO is higher than that of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which is associated with the coordination environments (such as tetrahedral (<i>T</i><sub>d</sub>), octahedral (O<sub>h</sub>), or square pyramidal (P<sub><i>y</i></sub>) geometry) and the local symmetry of V centers in the sample. Generally, <i>T</i><sub>d</sub> symmetry contributes to high pre-edge intensity, whereas apparently low intensities are observed for O<sub>h</sub> and P<sub><i>y</i></sub> coordination. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> As mentioned above, due to the elongation of the V–O distance in the c-axial direction, the V–O–V bilayer is split into two discrete V–O–V layers in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), and the V centers are <i>T</i><sub>d</sub> or P<sub><i>y</i></sub> coordinated with four or five oxygen atoms, leading to the higher pre-edge intensity of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO due to the appearance of the distorted <i>T</i><sub>d</sub> vanadium. By contrast, the lower pre-edge intensity of the commercial V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is due to the high symmetry of the orthorhombic V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (PDF #72-0433, <i>a</i> = 11.51 Å, <i>b</i> = 4.369 Å, <i>c</i> = 3.563 Å, α = β = γ = 90°). The result agrees with the aforementioned Rietveld refinement, in which the preintercalation of organic species into orthorhombic V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> can decrease the crystal symmetry to obtain triclinic (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><div class="NLM_p">As for the discharged and charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrodes, their XANES are similar to that of the pristine sample with subtle difference, suggesting the structural maintenance of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO during the discharge/charge process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b (inset), the main edge and pre-edge of the discharged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO slightly shift to lower energies and the pre-edge decreases in intensity in comparison with those of the charged counterpart, which is due to the reduction of V<sup>5+/4+</sup> → V<sup>4+/3+</sup> and the increased coordination number (4/5 → 5/6) of V centers during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, agreeing with the V 2p fine spectra and the previous works. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The change in the EXAFS shows a similar trend. Compared with the charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, the decrease in the amplitude of the first shell V–O for the discharged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO indicates the decreasing symmetry with the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). The result agrees with the <i>ex situ</i> XRD pattern, in which the insertion of an alien species would decrease the crystalline symmetry and lead to the amorphous (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO sample. Meanwhile, the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion mainly affects the first V–O shell structure, leading to a slight shift to higher radial distances, while the V–V shell structure only displays changes in intensity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c).</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to gain deep insight into the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism, we also measured <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN for comparison. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d, in the pristine (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode, the (001) diffraction facet of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is a bit positively shifted from 8.4 to 8.9° due to the presence of conducting agents and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) binder. However, during the first discharge/charge cycle, the (001) plane of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN is maintained during the whole process, which is different from the disappearance of the (001) reflection peak in the <i>ex situ</i> XRD pattern of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><div class="NLM_p">More importantly, the (001) diffraction peak has undergone a gradual positive shift (8.9 → 9.7°), indicating a slight contraction of the interlayer spacing during the discharge process, which is possibly due to an increased electrostatic interaction between the V–O–V layer and the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup>. And the charge process is reverse. However, this (001) diffraction peak has not completely shifted back to its initial position, indicating the partial retention of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interlayer spacing of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. This peak also matches well with the prominent (001) diffraction facet of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> such as Na<sub>0.56</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (PDF #79-2076) or Na<sub>0.64</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (PDF #89-0505) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d,e). Furthermore, upon the first discharge to 2.2 V, with the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, the (002) reflection peak of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (or Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) is observed at 17.8° (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d,e). Similar to the (001) facet, the (002) reflection peak shows a similar change trend during the discharge/charge process. The similar structure of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, <i>d</i><sub>(00<i>l</i>)</sub> (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) = <i>d</i><sub>(00<i>l</i>)</sub> ((<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN) indicates the partial topotactic phase transformation of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN → Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d,e). Topotactic phase transformation means that the involved two phases are matched in a lattice, which enables structural transition without remarkably changing the crystalline symmetry or principal framework of the parental phase, leading to the reversible (de)intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charge/discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 55 to 58">(55−58)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Impressively, it is found that extra diffraction peaks emerge at 29.4, 31.9, or 38.9° at the fully charged state (charge to 3.8 V), which can be indexed to NaNO<sub>2</sub> (PDF#71-0728) or NaNO<sub>3</sub> (PDF#70-1518) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d,e), indicating the transformation of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN → NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>. This phenomenon may be associated with a mixed-anion coordination effect induced by N-substitution, which shortens the V–O<sub>t</sub> bonds in the <i>c</i>-axial and leads to the shrinkage of the (001) interlayer spacing in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. Therefore, the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> ions would be coordinated with O<sub>t</sub>/N to generate the additional products of NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>. Such transformation corresponds to the extra oxidization peaks in the CVs of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9a</a>). And the irreversible transformation may explain the capacity degradation of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN during the cycling test.</div><div class="NLM_p">In an attempt to verify the above phenomena, <i>ex situ</i> SEM images, EDS, as well as elemental mappings of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrodes have also been measured. There is no obvious change in the morphology of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode during the first discharge/charge process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), whereas microflowers built by nanobelts are observed on the surface of the fully charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode, which are probably related to the generation of NaNO<sub>3</sub>/NaNO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Please see Section S5, Figures S10–S13 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> for details.</div><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i> IR spectra were also carried out to explore the change of the organic species in the two electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14a</a>,b). It can be seen that the transformation of R-C═O + e<sup>–</sup> + Na<sup>+</sup> → R–C–O-Na during the first discharge process. And the trend is reversed during the subsequent charge process. In addition, the characteristic peaks of the phenyl group can be detected during the whole discharge–charge process and even after 200 discharge–charge cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting the maintenance of the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species in the host framework of vanadium oxide, further proving the anchoring effect of the V–O–V layer.</div><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, <i>ex situ</i> XPS measurements were carried out to further explore the structural and chemical evolution of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode during the first discharge/charge process. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a> shows the survey spectra at different states, in which all of the peaks are well defined without impurity. The V 2p fine spectra are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a. When fully discharged to 1.0 V, the V<sup>5+</sup> signals become weaker, and the signals of V<sup>4+</sup> are intensified, accompanied with the appearance of the V<sup>3+</sup> signals (2p<sub>3/2</sub>: 515.5 eV; 2p<sub>1/2</sub>: 521.6 eV), indicating the reduction reactions of V<sup>5+</sup> → V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup> → V<sup>3+</sup> with the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. And the charge process is, on the contrary, indicating the oxidation reactions of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>→ V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a–e) <i>Ex situ</i> XPS spectra of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (f) (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrodes under different states: (a) V 2p, (b) O 1s, (c) C 1s, (d) Na 1s, (e) F 1s, and (f) N 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The O 1s spectra are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b. At the fully discharged state, the relative intensity of the C═O group becomes weaker along with the intensification of the C–O–H signal; meanwhile, two new signals appear at 529.2 and 536.1 eV, which are attributed to Na–O–V and C–O–Na, respectively. The reduction reactions of V–O + e<sup>–</sup> + Na<sup>+</sup> → V–O–Na and R-C═O + e<sup>–</sup> + Na<sup>+</sup> → R–C–O-Na can be responsible for the above phenomenon, further demonstrating that the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species can deliver extra capacity in addition to the vanadium oxide. And with the intercalation of the solvated Na<sup>+</sup>, partial oxygen vacancies are occupied by the solvated shell of Na<sup>+</sup>, and the percentage of oxygen defect (O<sub>d</sub>) decreases from 2.7 to 1.3%. The charge process is the opposite. It is worth noting that the signals of Na–O–V and C–O–Na can still be observed when fully charged to 3.8 V, suggesting the Na<sup>+</sup> ions can not be completely extracted from the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO electrode, which matches well with the EDS result.</div><div class="NLM_p">As exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c, in the C 1s fine spectra, the variation tendencies of the C═O and C–O signals are in agreement with those in the O 1s spectra. That is to say, the relative intensities of C═O and C–O signals show <i>quasi</i>-reversible shifts before and after Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. And the −CF signal (290.8 eV) at the discharged/charged electrodes originates from the PVDF binder.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na 1s fine spectra are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d, in which the characteristic peaks at ∼1070.8 and ∼1071.5 eV can be indexed to Na–O and Na–F, respectively, indicating that the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> would bind with O atoms from the V–O–V layer or the <i>p</i>-TQ organic species. The Na–F signal originates from the cathode–electrolyte interface (CEI). At the fully charged state, the relative intensity of Na–O is decreased, suggesting the partial deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, which agrees well with the EDS result.</div><div class="NLM_p">The appearance of the CEI (Na–F) can be further demonstrated by the F 1s spectra. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e, at the discharge and charge states, three peaks are detected at 684.4, 686.5, and 687.9 eV, corresponding to Na–F, P–F, and C–F, respectively. The P–F signal comes from the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. And the C–F signal is from the PVDF binder, which is consistent with the C 1s spectra. The formation of CEI (Na–F) can contribute to the fast reaction kinetics and excellent long-term durability of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO.</div><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i> XPS measurements of the (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode were also performed for comparison (for details, see section S7 and Figure S16 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">The most distinguished characteristics of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN can be detected by the N 1s fine spectra. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f, the pristine (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN powder displays two peaks at 400.3 (V–N–V) and 401.4 eV (V = N), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and the V = N signal is much stronger than the V–N–V signal, suggesting that the incorporated N atoms mainly replace the terminal O (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms from the V–O–V layer in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN. However, at the fully discharged state, the relative intensity of V–N–V is dominant over that of V = N, which may be due to the binding of N with the inserted Na. And at the fully charged state, the retention of the dominant V–N–V signal suggests that the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> is partially irreversible, agreeing with the aforementioned <i>ex situ</i> XRD pattern, in which Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is detected at the fully charged (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN electrode. Furthermore, two new N signals emerge at 403.2 and 407.3 eV at the fully charged state, which are attributed to NaNO<sub>2</sub> and NaNO<sub>3</sub>, respectively, suggesting the transformation of N(O)═V + Na<sup>+</sup> → NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>, which is consistent with the XRD and Na 1s data.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> DFT Simulations</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to illustrate the effect of the N-substitution on the organic species-inserted vanadate, DFT simulations were performed. First, the total density of states (DOS) was comparatively calculated, and projected DOS (PDOS) was comparatively calculated for the unit cells of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with and without N-substitution. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17(a,b)</a>, it can be seen that whether partial oxygen atoms from the V–O–V layer were substituted by nitrogen atoms or not, their total DOS (TDOS) near the Fermi level are mainly constructed by V 3d, O/N 2p, and C 2p states. And more electrons cross the Fermi level of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a), indicating that the N-substituted sample possesses better electron conductivity than the free-N counterpart.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/medium/ic3c02974_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) TDOS of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with and without N-substitution, V 3d, O 2p, and N 2p PDOS of (b) (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> and (c) (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, in which the position of the Fermi level is indicated by a black dotted vertical line at 0 eV; (d) V <i>d</i>-band and O <i>p</i>-band centers in N-substituted and free-N samples; molecular orbital energy levels and energy differences (Δ<i>E</i>) based on V 3d orbitals in (e) {VO<sub>6</sub>} and (f) {VO<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>}/{VO<sub>6</sub>} O<sub>h</sub> geometries.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/asset/images/large/ic3c02974_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to gain deeper insight into the enhanced electron conductivity caused by N-substitution, the PDOS on V 3d and -O 2p is further compared in the absence and presence of N-substitution. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>(b,c), more V 3d and O 2p electrons are observed near the Fermi level of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> than those of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. And the V 3d and O 2p states in the N-incorporated sample are better overlapped near the Fermi level than those in the free-N counterpart (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b,c), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a> suggesting that <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization near the Fermi level in the N-substituted sample are stronger than those in the free-N sample. It means that the N-substitution leads to enhanced <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> interaction near the Fermi level, which is beneficial for the effective electron transfer between O 2p and 3d orbitals, thus giving rise to better electron conductivity. Meanwhile, in the N-substituted sample, the V 3d and N 2p states near the Fermi energy are also well overlapped (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b), suggesting that effective <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization also exists between the V and N atoms. As mentioned above, in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub>, partial terminal oxygen (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms from the V–O–V layer are substituted by N atoms, leading to the shortened V–O<sub>t</sub>/N distances in the <i>c</i>-axial direction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), which results from the effective <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridizations of V 3<i>d</i>-N 2<i>p</i> and V 3<i>d</i>- O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i>.</div><div class="NLM_p">And the effective V 3<i>d</i>-O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i> orbital hybridization can be unveiled by Bader charge analyses. It is calculated that the average Bader charge over the O<sub>t</sub> atoms from the V–O–V layer in the N-substituted sample is −0.869 |e|, which is more negative than that (−0.800 |e|) in the free-N sample, suggesting more electron density around the O<sub>t</sub> atoms in the presence of N-substitution. Therefore, the enhanced orbital hybridization of V 3<i>d</i> and O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i> induced by the substitution of N lead to the intensified V–O<sub>t</sub> bonds. As a consequence, mixed-anion coordination effects with compressed octahedral geometries are observed for the V centers in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The <i>d</i>-band center of the V atoms and the <i>p</i>-band center of the O atoms in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with and without N-substitution have been calculated comparatively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d, in the presence of N-substitution, the <i>d</i>-band and <i>p</i>-band centers are at 1.15 and −1.62 eV, respectively, which both shift toward the Fermi level in comparison with the values (−0.09/–2.41 eV) in the free-N sample, leading to enhanced <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization near the Fermi level in the presence of N-incorporation.</div><div class="NLM_p">The richer redox reactions in the N-incorporated sample are also associated with the <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization. Based on the crystal-field theory, the energy levels of V 3d orbitals in the O<sub>h</sub> field can be split into high-energy e<sub>g</sub> orbits and low-energy t<sub>2g</sub> orbits (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>). And the splitting energy of V 3d orbitals in the {VO<sub>6</sub>} chemical environment differs from that of V in the {VO<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>} geometry. In the absence of N-substitution, the molecular orbital energy diagram of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>e. The O 2p-V 3d orbital hybridization leads to a set of bonding orbits such as σ(e<sub>g</sub>)/π(t<sub>2g</sub>) and antibonding orbits such as σ(e<sub>g</sub>)*/π(t<sub>2g</sub>)*, thus giving rise to a set of energy differences based on V<sup>3+</sup>/<sup>4+</sup> (Δ<i>E</i><sub>1</sub>) and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>(Δ<i>E</i><sub>2</sub>) in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the discharge/charge process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>e). However, in the presence of N-substitution, the N 2p energy level is introduced near the O 2p level (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>f). And the N 2p-V 3d orbital hybridization results in another set of bonding orbits such as σ′(e<sub>g</sub>)/π′(t<sub>2g</sub>) and antibonding orbits such as σ′(e<sub>g</sub>)*/π′(t<sub>2g</sub>)* (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>f), thus leading to another set of energy changes based on V<sup>3+</sup>/<sup>4+</sup> (Δ<i>E</i><sub>1</sub><sup>′</sup>) and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>(Δ<i>E</i><sub>2</sub><sup>′</sup>) during the discharge/charge process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>f). These two sets of orbital energy levels and energy differences coexist in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> due to the presence of {VO<sub>6</sub>} and {VO<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>} octahedra, giving rise to richer redox reactions with enhanced capacity in the N-substituted (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> than in the free-N (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">More electron densities around the terminal O (O<sub>t</sub>) atoms in the N-substituted sample can facilitate the binding toward the inserted Na<sup>+</sup>, which is verified by the DFT calculation of binding energy. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>, the binding energies of the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub> are more negative than those in the free-N sample (see section S8 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> for details). Consequently, the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the N-substituted sample are more difficult to be extracted out, leading to the partial accumulation of Na<sup>+</sup> in the N-substituted sample, which can account for the partial phase transformation of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN into NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub> and capacity fading in the subsequent cycling test.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In the present work, (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·0.38H<sub>2</sub>O, denoted as (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO, was synthesized via a facile hydrothermal reaction between V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and <i>p</i>-HTQ, whereas in the presence of VN and <i>p</i>-HTQ, (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(O/N)<sub>5</sub>, denoted as (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, was prepared. The lattice spacings of the (001) facets in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN are 13.7 and 10.7 Å, respectively. 3D cRED, Rietveld refinement, and HAADF-STEM of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO and (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN reveal the successful insertion of <i>p</i>-TQ into the layered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. The (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VO sample exhibits an excellent electrochemical performance when used as the SIB cathode, whereas (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN displays a significantly improved capacity at a smaller current density with the maintenance of the structural integrity as well as the partial topotactic phase conversion into Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> during the discharge/charge process. In (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, partial O<sub>t</sub> atoms from the V–O–V layer have been replaced by N atoms, which can boost the orbital hybridization of V 3<i>d</i> and O<sub>t</sub> 2<i>p</i> and then shorten the V–O<sub>t</sub> bonds in the <i>c</i>-axial direction with the elongation of the V–O bonds in the <i>ab</i> plane, leading to mixed-anion coordination effect with compressed octahedra and the shrinkage of the interlayer spacing. The enhanced <i>d</i>-<i>p</i> orbital hybridization also causes enhanced electron densities around the O<sub>t</sub> atoms, which can facilitate the affinity toward Na<sup>+</sup> in (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, giving rise to the partial accumulation of Na<sup>+</sup> and phase conversion into NaNO<sub>2</sub>/NaNO<sub>3</sub>. Furthermore, the N-substitution can improve electron conductivity with more molecular orbital energy levels, thus leading to multistep redox reactions and enhanced capacity, as evidenced by DFT simulations.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="CCDCDropzone1" data-pb-dropzone="CCDCDropzone1"><div data-ccdc-widget-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974" data-ccdc-widget-width="100%" data-ccdc-widget-style="ballandstick" data-ccdc-widget-labels="none" data-ccdc-widget-packing="unitcell" data-ccdc-widget-measure="distance" data-ccdc-widget-hydrogens="on" data-ccdc-widget-spinning="on" data-ccdc-widget-disorder="off" id="ccdc-widget-container-0" class="ccdc-widget" style="width: 100%; min-height: 372px; display: none;"><div class="ccdc-widget-viewer-container" style="height: 372px;">

</div><div class="ccdc-widget-0-error-panel" style="text-align: center"><p><img src="https://cdn.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/content/images/ccdc-branding-logo-strapline.svg" alt="CCDC Logo" style="width: 200px"></p><p>No structures are currently available to view but they are available from the CCDC website <a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/structures" target="_blank">www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/structures</a></p></div></div><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM and EDS as well as elemental mappings; XRD patterns, TG curves, XPS, GCD, and CV curves; electrochemical kinetics analysis of (<i>p</i>-TQ)-VN, IR spectra, TDOS, and PDOS plots; splitting scheme of V 3d orbitals; the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> in the inner channels of (<i>p</i>-TQ)<sub>0.22</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with and without N-substitution, tables and other Supporting Information (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974/suppl_file/ic3c02974_si_001.pdf">ic3c02974_si_001.pdf (2.54 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Accession Codes</span><p class="last">CCDC <a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structure_request?pid=ccdc:2277625&amp;issn=0020-1669&amp;id=doi:10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;sid=ACS">2277625</a>–<a href="https://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/services/structure_request?pid=ccdc:2277626&amp;issn=0020-1669&amp;id=doi:10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974&amp;sid=ACS">2277626</a> contain the supplementary crystallographic data for this paper. These data can be obtained free of charge via <a href="http://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/data_request/cif" class="ext-link">www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/data_request/cif</a>, by emailing <a href="mailto:data_request@ccdc.cam.ac.uk">data_request@ccdc.cam.ac.uk</a>, or by contacting The Cambridge Crystallographic Data Centre, 12 Union Road, Cambridge CB2 1EZ, UK; fax: +44 1223 336033.</p><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c02974" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Promises and challenges of next-generation "beyond lithium-ion" batteries for electric vehicles and grid decarbonization</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tian, Yaosen; Zeng, Guobo; Rutt, Ann; Shi, Tan; Kim, Haegyeom; Wang, Jingyang; Koettgen, Julius; Sun, Yingzhi; Ouyang, Bin; Chen, Tina; Lun, Zhengyan; Rong, Ziqin; Persson, Kristin; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">121</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The tremendous improvement in performance and cost of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have made them the technol. of choice for elec. energy storage.  While established battery chemistries and cell architectures for Li-ion batteries achieve good power and energy d., LIBs are unlikely to meet all the performance, cost, and scaling targets required for energy storage, in particular, in large-scale applications such as electrified transportation and grids.  The demand to further reduce cost and/or increase energy d., as well as the growing concern related to natural resource needs for Li-ion have accelerated the investigation of so-called "beyond Li-ion" technologies.  In this review, we will discuss the recent achievements, challenges, and opportunities of four important "beyond Li-ion" technologies: Na-ion batteries, K-ion batteries, all-solid-state batteries, and multivalent batteries.  The fundamental science behind the challenges, and potential solns. toward the goals of a low-cost and/or high-energy-d. future, are discussed in detail for each technol.  While it is unlikely that any given new technol. will fully replace Li-ion in the near future, "beyond Li-ion" technologies should be thought of as opportunities for energy storage to grow into mid/large-scale applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.0c02181"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abraham, K.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Sodium-Ion Battery Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongjin; Xiao, Lifen; Chen, Zhongxue; Ai, Xinping; Cao, Yuliang; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8489</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Grid-scale energy storage systems with low-cost and high-performance electrodes are needed to meet the requirements of sustainable energy systems.  Due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources and their similar electrochem. to the established lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as ideal candidates for grid-scale energy storage systems.  In the past decade, though tremendous efforts have been made to promote the development of SIBs, and significant advances have been achieved, further improvements are still required in terms of energy/power d. and long cyclic stability for commercialization.  In this review, the latest progress in electrode materials for SIBs, including a variety of promising cathodes and anodes, is briefly summarized.  Besides, the sodium storage mechanisms, endeavors on electrochem. property enhancements, structural and compositional optimizations, challenges and perspectives of the electrode materials for SIBs are discussed.  Though enormous challenges may lie ahead, we believe that through intensive research efforts, sodium-ion batteries with low operation cost and longevity will be commercialized for large-scale energy storage application in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202108384"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Prussian blue analogues for sodium-ion batteries: Past, Present, and Future</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prussian Blue Analogues for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Past, Present, and Future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Jian; Zhang, Wang; Liu, Qiannan; Wang, Jiazhao; Chou, Shulei; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) have attracted wide attention for their application in the energy storage and conversion field due to their low cost, facile synthesis, and appreciable electrochem. performance.  At the present stage, most research on PBAs is focused on their material-level optimization, whereas their properties in practical battery systems are seldom considered.  This review aims to first provide an overview of the history and parameters of PBA materials and analyze the fundamental principles toward rational design of PBAs, and then evaluate the prospects and challenges for PBAs for practical sodium-ion batteries, hoping to bridge the gap between lab. research and com. reality.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-020-20169-1"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Girard, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The invention of rechargeable batteries has dramatically changed our landscapes and lives, underpinning the explosive worldwide growth of consumer electronics, ushering in an unprecedented era of elec. vehicles, and potentially paving the way for a much greener energy future.  Unfortunately, current battery technologies suffer from a no. of challenges, e.g., capacity loss and failure upon prolonged cycling, limited ion diffusion kinetics, and a rather sparse palette of high-performing electrode materials.  Here, we discuss the origins of diffusion limitations in oxide materials using V2O5 as a model system.  In particular, we discuss constrictions in ionic conduction pathways, narrow energy dispersion of conduction band states, and the stabilization and self-trapping of polarons as local phenomena that have substantial implications for introducing multiscale compositional and phase heterogeneities.  Strategies for mitigating such limitations are discussed such as reducing diffusion path lengths and the design of metastable frameworks yielding frustrated coordination and decreased barriers for migration of polarons.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2017.06.093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Design of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·xH<sub>2</sub>O cathode for highly enhancing sodium storage</span>. <i>J. In this work, we report the electrochem. insertion of sodium in high pressure β-V2O5 and its performance as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The material shows a first discharge capacity of 132 mAh g-1 in the 3.6-2.0 V range at a C/20 c.d. and a max. capacity of 147 mAh g-1 under equil. conditions, corresponding to the insertion of 1 Na+ ion per formula unit. Concerning the reaction mechanism of high pressure β-V2O5 upon sodium insertion/de-insertion, several phase transitions are inferred from the voltage-compn. profile.  Ex situ XRD reveals the appearance of several NaxV2O5 phases in the 0 ≤ x ≤ 1 compositional range, which are closely related with the non-sodiated high pressure β-V2O5 structure.  An irreversible structural transformation occurs during the very first inserted sodium, at the beginning of the first discharge, and the original high pressure β-V2O5 structure is not recovered upon full Na+ extn.  Electrochem. performances are outstanding though.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.mtener.2018.12.003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosting the cycling stability of hydrated vanadium pentoxide by Y<sup>3+</sup> pillaring for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Materials Today Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">218</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">227</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hydrated V2O5 has attracted considerable attention for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high theor. capacity.  However, the poor cycling performance caused by structural instability during sodiaton/desodiation greatly hampers its application.  Herein, Y3+ pre-intercalated hydrated V2O5 samples (YxV2O5, x = 0.0, 0.02 and 0.06) are synthesized by a facile sol-gel and freeze-drying routes followed by heat treatment in air at 200 °C.  As cathode material for SIBs, the Y0·02V2O5 sample exhibits much enhanced cycling stability, higher Na+ diffusion coeff., lower electrochem. reaction resistance, and improved rate capability compared to the pure V2O5 counterpart.  First-principle calcns. reveals that the pre-intercalated Y3+ forms [YO6] pillar with two oxygen atoms from the VO5 pyramids and four oxygen atoms from the intercalated water mols., which firmly binds the V2O5 double layers together.  Ex-situ XRD, SEM, and TEM anal. demonstrate that Y3+ pre-intercalation effectively strengthens the structural integrity, stabilizes the layered structure, and suppress the irreversible phase transition of hydrated V2O5 during repeated discharge/charge cycling, and therefore leading to enhanced cycling stability and improved rate capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201902093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baddour-Hadjean, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thanh Nguyen Huynh, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Batyrbekuly, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bach, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pereira-Ramos, J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Bilayered Potassium Vanadate K0.5V2O5 as Superior Cathode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Baddour-Hadjean, Rita; Huynh, Le Thanh Nguyen; Batyrbekuly, Daureen; Bach, Stephane; Pereira-Ramos, Jean-Pierre</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A bilayered potassium vanadate K0.5V2O5 (KVO) is synthesized by a fast and facile synthesis route and evaluated as a pos. electrode material for Na-ion batteries.  Half the potassium ions can be topotactically extd. from KVO through the first charge, allowing 1.14 Na+ ions to be reversibly inserted.  A good rate capability is also highlighted, with 160 mAh g-1 at C/10, 94 mAh g-1 at C/2, 73 mAh g-1 at 2C and excellent cycling stability with 152 mAh g-1 still available after 50 cycles at C/10.  Ex situ X-ray diffraction reveals weak and reversible structural changes resulting in soft breathing of the KVO host lattice upon Na extn.-insertion cycles (ΔV/V≈3 %).  A high structure stability upon cycling is also achieved, at both the long-range order and at. scale probed by Raman spectroscopy.  This remarkable behavior is ascribed to the large interlayer spacing of KVO (≈9.5 Å) stabilized by pillar K ions, which is able to accommodate Na ions without any crit. change to the structure.  Kinetics measurements reveal a good Na diffusivity that is hardly affected upon discharge.  This study opens an avenue for further exploration of potassium vanadates and other bronzes in the field of Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.06.021"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-pot hydrothermal synthesis of Na<sub>x</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O/KB nanocomposite as a sodium-ion battery cathode for improved reversible capacity and rate performance</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tuning the Kinetics of Zinc-Ion Insertion/Extraction in V2O5 by In Situ Polyaniline Intercalation Enables Improved Aqueous Zinc-Ion Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Sucheng; Zhu, He; Zhang, Binghao; Li, Gen; Zhu, Hekang; Ren, Yang; Geng, Hongbo; Yang, Yang; Liu, Qi; Li, Cheng Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs) are emerging as a promising alternative for Li-ion batteries.  However, the developed cathodes suffer from sluggish Zn2+ diffusion kinetics, leading to poor rate capability and inadequate cycle life.  Herein, an in situ polyaniline (PANI) intercalation strategy is developed to facilitate the Zn2+ (de)intercalation kinetics in V2O5.  In this way, a remarkably enlarged interlayer distance (13.90 Å) can be constructed alternatively between the V-O layers, offering expediting channels for facile Zn2+ diffusion.  Importantly, the electrostatic interactions between the Zn2+ and the host O2-, which is another key factor in hindering the Zn2+ diffusion kinetics, can be effectively blocked by the unique π-conjugated structure of PANI.  As a result, the PANI-intercalated V2O5 exhibits a stable and highly reversible electrochem. reaction during repetitive Zn2+ insertion and extn., as demonstrated by in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction and Raman studies.  Further first-principles calcns. clearly reveal a remarkably lowered binding energy between Zn2+ and host O2-, which explains the favorable kinetics in PANI-intercalated V2O5.  Benefitting from the above, the overall electrochem. performance of PANI-intercalated V2O5 electrode is remarkable improved, exhibiting excellent high rate capability of 197.1 mAh g-1 at c.d. of 20 A g-1 with capacity retention of 97.6% over 2000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.colsurfa.2023.131703"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ran, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Preparation of polyaniline intercalated V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> composites and study of their self-charging mechanism</span>. <i>Colloids Surf., A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>671</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">131703</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Vanadium oxides are an important aq. zinc ion battery cathode material with the advantage of abundant reserves and high specific capacity.  However, the strong interaction between the zinc ions and the main lattice of the material results in poor cycling stability and multiplicative performance of the cell.  In this paper, we propose a mol. pre-embedding modification strategy for in situ intercalation of polyaniline.  Flower cluster-like org.-inorg. (PANI-V2O5, PVO) hybrid materials with adjustable layer spacing were synthesized by simultaneous oxidative polymn. using vanadate and aniline as precursors without the introduction of inactive cations.  PVO-60 exhibits a large specific capacity (391.1 mAh·g-1 at a c.d. of 0.2 A·g-1).  The results of d. flooding calcns. demonstrate that the polyaniline layer blocks the electrostatic interaction between Zn2+ and the V2O5 backbone, thus promoting Zn2+ kinetics.  Drawing on the air battery structure, a battery device that allows for air self-charging was designed using PVO-60 as the pos. electrode.  This work not only provides ideas for the design of vanadium oxides, but also offers the prospect of broadening the application of zinc ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2022.139734"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Long-lifespan benzoquinone-intercalated vanadium oxide with vacancies and disorders on the (00l) facets for efficient sodium-ion battery: A facile approach to Na<sup>+</sup> capture and pre-sodiation</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Defect Engineering on Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yiqiong; Tao, Li; Xie, Chao; Wang, Dongdong; Zou, Yuqin; Chen, Ru; Wang, Yanyong; Jia, Chuankun; Wang, Shuangyin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The reasonable design of electrode materials for rechargeable batteries plays an important role in promoting the development of renewable energy technol.  With the in-depth understanding of the mechanisms underlying electrode reactions and the rapid development of advanced technol., the performance of batteries has significantly been optimized through the introduction of defect engineering on electrode materials.  A large no. of coordination unsatd. sites can be exposed by defect construction in electrode materials, which play a crucial role in electrochem. reactions.  Herein, recent advances regarding defect engineering in electrode materials for rechargeable batteries are systematically summarized, with a special focus on the application of metal-ion batteries, lithium-sulfur batteries, and metal-air batteries.  The defects can not only effectively promote ion diffusion and charge transfer but also provide more storage/adsorption/active sites for guest ions and intermediate species, thus improving the performance of batteries.  Moreover, the existing challenges and future development prospects are forecast, and the electrode materials are further optimized through defect engineering to promote the development of the battery industry.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.0c00080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, J.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polymer-Derived Heteroatom-Doped Porous Carbon Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Hong; Shao, Yue; Mei, Shilin; Lu, Yan; Zhang, Miao; Sun, Jian-ke; Matyjaszewski, Krzysztof; Antonietti, Markus; Yuan, Jiayin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">120</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9363-9419</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatom-doped porous carbon materials (HPCMs) have found extensive applications in adsorption/sepn., org. catalysis, sensing, and energy conversion/storage.  The judicious choice of carbon precursors is crucial for the manuf. of HPCMs with specific usages and maximization of their functions.  In this regard, polymers as precursors have demonstrated great promise because of their versatile mol. and nanoscale structures, modulatable chem. compn., and rich processing techniques to generate textures that, in combination with proper solid-state chem., can be maintained throughout carbonization.  This Review comprehensively surveys the progress in polymer-derived functional HPCMs in terms of how to produce and control their porosities, heteroatom doping effects, and morphologies and their related use.  First, we summarize and discuss synthetic approaches, including hard and soft templating methods as well as direct synthesis strategies employing polymers to control the pores and/or heteroatoms in HPCMs.  Second, we summarize the heteroatom doping effects on the thermal stability, electronic and optical properties, and surface chem. of HPCMs.  Specifically, the heteroatom doping effect, which involves both single-type heteroatom doping and codoping of two or more types of heteroatoms into the carbon network, is discussed.  Considering the significance of the morphologies of HPCMs in their application spectrum, potential choices of suitable polymeric precursors and strategies to precisely regulate the morphologies of HPCMs are presented.  Finally, we provide our perspective on how to predefine the structures of HPCMs by using polymers to realize their potential applications in the current fields of energy generation/conversion and environmental remediation. However, bulk TMNs usually suffer from limited nos. of active sites and sluggish ionic kinetics, and eventually ordinary electrochem. performance.     </div><div class="casAbstract">During the last century, inorganic oxide compounds laid foundations for materials synthesis, characterization, and technology translation by adding new functions into devices previously dominated by main-group element semiconductor compounds.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Oxynitrides were explored extensively in the past decade because of their interesting properties, such as visible-light absorption, photocatalytic activity and high dielec. permittivity.  Their synthesis typically requires high-temp. NH3 treatment (800-1,300°) of precursors, such as oxides, but the highly reducing conditions and the low mobility of N3- species in the lattice place significant constraints on the compn. and structure-and hence the properties-of the resulting oxynitrides.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anion order in perovskite oxynitrides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang Minghui; Oro-Sole Judith; Rodgers Jennifer A; Jorge Ana Belen; Fuertes Amparo; Attfield J Paul</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature chemistry</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition-metal oxynitrides with perovskite-type structures are an emerging class of materials with optical, photocatalytic, dielectric and magnetoresistive properties that may be sensitive to oxide-nitride order, but the anion-ordering principles were unclear.  Here we report an investigation of the representative compounds SrMO(2)N (M = Nb, Ta) using neutron and electron diffraction.  This revealed a robust 1O/2(O(0.5)N(0.5)) partial anion order (up to at least 750 °C in the apparently cubic high-temperature phases) that directs the rotations of MO(4)N(2) octahedra in the room-temperature superstructure.  The anion distribution is consistent with local cis-ordering of the two nitrides in each octahedron driven by covalency, which results in disordered zigzag M-N chains in planes within the perovskite lattice.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Vanadium oxide (V2O5) nanotubes and polyethylene glycol (PEG) surfactant V2O5 nanotubes were synthesized using simple hydrothermal process.  The electrochem. performance of these nanostructures was investigated for the application of Li batteries.  Microstructure and morphol. of the samples were studied by XRD, FTIR, FE-SEM and TEM anal.  The results showed that the H atoms of PEG are hydrogen bonded with the O atoms in V=O bonds of the V2O5, which effectively shielded against electrostatic interaction between the V2O5 interlayer and Li+ ions.  The battery of V2O5 nanotubes electrode showed initial specific capacity 192 mAhg-1, whereas the PEG surfactant V2O5 nanotubes exhibited 204 mAhg-1.  It was found that PEG surfactant V2O5 nanotubes material showed high specific capacity at initial stages besides better stability was exhibited at higher cycle nos. when compared to V2O5 nanotubes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The ever-growing portable electronics and elec. vehicle draws the attention of scaling up of energy storage systems with high areal-capacity.  The concept of thick electrode designs has been used to improve the active mass loading toward achieving high overall energy d.  However, the poor rate capabilities of electrode material owing to increasing electrode thickness significantly affect the rapid transportation of ionic and electron diffusion kinetics.  Herein, a new concept named "sub-thick electrodes" is successfully introduced to mitigate the Li-ion storage performance of electrodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The authors discuss the advantages of performing ESR on coordination and metalloorg. complexes contg. various oxidn. states of vanadium, from +4 to +2, using the high-frequency and -field version of this technique, known as HFEPR.  Thus, HFEPR is advantageous in investigating (S = 1/2) V(IV) species due to its higher g-factor resoln. over conventional EPR, albeit at the price of losing the hyperfine structure resoln. due to g-strain.  It is very much desirable in characterizing (S = 3/2) V(II) complexes since it is capable of precisely and accurately measuring the zero-field splitting present in these species.  Finally, HFEPR is indispensable in investigating (S = 1) V(III) species, which in conventional conditions are often "EPR-silent" due to the combination of their large zero-field splitting and integer spin no.  The use of HFEPR thus can provide addnl. information regarding the oxidn. state of the metal and the coordination geometry.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1039/D2CC04721G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The small energy d. and chemomech. degrdn. of layered manganese oxide limit practical application to sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Typically, Na2Mn3O7 shows a low redox plateau at 2.1 V vs. Na/Na+, and the oxygen redox reaction at a high voltage causes structural collapse.  Herein, a Na vacancy-induced boron doping strategy is demonstrated to improve the properties.  Boron is incorporated into selective sites in the lattice in the center of the MnO6 octahedral ring at the O-layer.  Bonding of boron in the TM layer enhances the electrochem. activity of low-valence Mn, giving rise to two reversible redox peaks at 2.45 and 2.55 V to enhance the av. redox voltage.  At the same time, the O 2p chem. state becomes weaker around the Fermi level, thus suppressing oxygen overoxidn. for the high charge state and strengthening the layered structure during the redox reactions.  The reduced Mn-O covalency and small diffusion barrier energy stemming from bonding of boron in the oxygen layer produce excellent rate characteristics.  Modulation of the Mn 3d and O 2p orbital in Na2Mn3O7 by Na vacancies leads to selective doping of boron at different sites, and our results reveal that it is an important strategy for studying transition-metal-oxide-layered electrode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2003.08.028"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Noguchi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoshio, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis and electrochemical studies on Li–Mn–O compounds prepared at high temperatures</span>. <i>J. However, the sluggish kinetics stemming from the strong electrostatic interaction of divalent zinc ions in the host crystal structure is one of challenges for highly efficient energy storage. TiO2 (anatase) was studied over a dimensional regime where both capacitive and Li intercalation processes contribute to the total stored charge.  An anal. of the voltammetric sweep data was used to distinguish between the amt. of charge stored by these 2 processes.  At particle sizes &lt;10 nm, capacitive contributions became increasingly important, leading to greater amts. of total stored charge (gravimetrically normalized) with decreasing TiO2 particle size.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs) with high energy densities appear promising to meet the increasing demand for safe and sustainable energy storage devices.  However, electrode research on this low-cost and green system are faced with stiff challenges of identifying materials that permit divalent ion-intercalation/deintercalation.  Herein, we present layered-type LiV3O8 (LVO) as a prospective intercalation cathode for zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs) with high storage capacities.  The detailed phase evolution study during Zn intercalation using electrochem., in situ XRD, and simulation techniques reveals the large presence of a single-phase domain that proceeds via a stoichiometric ZnLiV3O8 phase to reversible solid-soln. ZnyLiV3O8 (y &gt; 1) phase.  The unique behavior, which is different from the reaction with lithium, contributes to high specific capacities of 172 mAh g-1 and amts. to 75% retention of the max. capacity achieved in 65 cycles with 100% Coulombic efficiency at a c.d. of 133 mA g-1.  The remarkable performance makes the development of this low-cost and safe battery technol. very promising, and this study also offers opportunities to enhance the understanding on electrochem. induced metastable phases for energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.0c04669"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sarapulova, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ehrenberg, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In operando synchrotron studies of NH4<sup>+</sup> preintercalated V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.nH<sub>2</sub>O nanobelts as the cathode material for aqueous rechargeable zinc batteries</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11809</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11820</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unlocking the Capacity of Vanadium Oxide by Atomically Thin Graphene-Analogous V2O5·nH2O in Aqueous Zinc-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Danyang; Wang, Xiaoying; Zhang, Wenming; Zhang, Yijing; Lei, Yu; Huang, Xintang; Zhu, Qiancheng; Liu, Jinping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
